        <script defer>
window.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    function getAlternateLanguageLink(currentPath) {
      const parts = currentPath.split('/').filter(Boolean);

      let isJapanese = false;
      let basePath = '';
      let slug = [];

      if (parts[0] === 'ja') {
        isJapanese = true;
        basePath = parts[1];
        slug = parts.slice(2);
      } else {
        basePath = parts[0];
        slug = parts.slice(1);
      }

      const newPath = isJapanese
        ? `/${basePath}/${slug.join('/')}`
        : `/ja/${basePath}/${slug.join('/')}`;

      return `https://www.kelacyber.com${newPath}`;
    }

    function updateURLs() {
      const currentPath = window.location.pathname;
      const jaLink = document.querySelector('#menu-item-wpml-ls-2-ja a');
      const enLink = document.querySelector('#menu-item-wpml-ls-2-en a');

      if (jaLink) {
        const enPath = currentPath.replace(/^\/ja/, '') || '/';
        jaLink.href = getAlternateLanguageLink(enPath);
      }

      if (enLink) {
        enLink.href = getAlternateLanguageLink(currentPath);
      }
    }

    let currentUrl = `${window.location.pathname}${window.location.search}`;
    let currentRef = document.referrer;
    const hook = (_this, method, callback) => {
        const orig = _this[method];

        return (...args) => {
          callback.apply(null, args);

          return orig.apply(_this, args);
        };
    };

    const handlePush = (state, title, url) => {
      if (!url) return;

      currentRef = currentUrl;
      const newUrl = url.toString();

      if (newUrl.substring(0, 4) === "http") {
        currentUrl = "/" + newUrl.split("/").splice(3).join("/");
      } else {
        currentUrl = newUrl;
      }

      console.log(`handlePush currentUrl`, currentUrl);

      if (currentUrl !== currentRef) {
        updateURLs();
      }
    };
    history.pushState = hook(history, "pushState", handlePush);
    history.replaceState = hook(history, "replaceState", handlePush); 

    updateURLs(); 
  });
</script>

class="ent-tss-o5kd66-enContentEditor en-content"><div class="ent-tss-9lqj9g-editable-editable" data-slate-editor="true" data-slate-node="value" contenteditable="false" zindex="-1" class="MuiTypography-root MuiTypography-body2 ent-css-uwbx"><span> Updated <!-- -->February 21, 2020 </span></p></div><div class="ent-tss-8gyp5s-postedTimeCommentsWrapper"></div><div class="ent-tss-wdg0eu-mainImageWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img loading="eager" fetchpriority="high" id="ent-blog-image" class="ent-tss-32jtuo-previewCover-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x680/kela_1-1741634412114.jpg" alt="a computer screen with the word war of the cult on it" width="680" height="383"><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-1vclqo4-expandIcon ent-tss-44-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div></div><div class="ent-tss-g7g134-blogContentWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-o5kd66-enContentEditor en-content"><div id="ent-blog-content" class="ent-tss-16bdfpw-editable" data-slate-editor="true" data-slate-node="value" contenteditable="false" zindex="-1" style="position:relative;outline:none;white-space:pre-wrap;word-wrap:break-word"><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="bottom-line-up-front"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Bottom Line Up Front</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This is the first post in a series of posts reviewing the supply chain of the Genesis Store market – a likely-Russian threat actor operating a successful, borderline innovative, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">pay-per-bot</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> store since 2018. The following post features a quick-and-easy methodology breaking down over 335,000 unique Genesis infections into four malware groups, allowing us to attribute </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">over 300,000 AZORult infections to the Genesis actors</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">currently involved in campaigns resulting is tens of thousands of new AZORult infections per month</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. Furthermore, it seems Genesis isn’t necessarily leading these campaigns, but rather working with various Malware-as-a-Service (MaaS) providers and cybercrime services.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This discovery, linking Genesis with widely known commodity malware, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">highlights the ongoing threat to organizations and the proliferation of illegal data obtained from infections</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. It also sheds light on the supply chain relationships between actors operating within the cybercrime financial ecosystem (read: Dark Net); we’ll explore this theme, including specific actors and trends, in the next posts.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="preface"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Preface</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Much has been written about the Genesis Store: the threat it poses to organizations, the native </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.kaspersky.com/about/press-releases/2019_meet-genesis-the-underground-e-shop-with-tens-of-thousands-of-digital-doppelgangers" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">fingerprinting abilities</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> embedded in the tools it provides, or (shameless plug alert) the </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://kelacyber.com/one-attackers-trash-is-another-attackers-treasure/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">shift in cybercrime business models</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> it represents.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">On a day-to-day operations basis, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">KELA automatically monitors new listings on the market</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> – allowing our clients to monitor relevant infections. While these real-time alerts allow threat intelligence or incident response teams to remediate relevant threats, many of our clients were interested in a wider, more contextual understanding of Genesis. For example, </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://intsights.com/resources/research-report-digital-browser-identities" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">one report indicated</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> that Genesis </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">“introduced a new breed of stealers specifically designed to collect digital fingerprints and artifacts;”</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> another referred to the Genesis</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true"> “botnet.”</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> That raises the question, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">what do Genesis actors actually do</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">? Develop specialized stealers, manage a MaaS botnet, or sell credentials obtained by other actors?</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Seeking to demystify the threat, we focused on three major areas:</span></span></span></p><ol data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-pw0qn0-numberedList"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Technical abilities</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> – which trojan is being used: commodity malware or a tailored tool?</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Operational independence</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> – are the Genesis actors leading independent campaigns, determining targets and infection methods, or reselling data obtained by other parties?</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Scale</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> – the spread, infection rate and profits pocketed by the actors</span></span></span></li></ol><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Our research aims to evaluate the actionable threat level: if ACME Corp found an infected endpoint offered for sale on the market, what should be the next steps of the incident response team? What does a Genesis infection say about further threat hunting, threat containment and lateral movement, or impending payloads? In the age of cybercrime </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.cybereason.com/blog/one-two-punch-emotet-trickbot-and-ryuk-steal-then-ransom-data" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">inter-group relations</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> and joint campaigns, understanding how Genesis actors position themselves in the cybercrime financial ecosystem as an emerging threat group can be extremely helpful to defenders.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Luckily, it’s our business to have access to large cybercrime-related data; as such, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">KELA’s systems have been scraping Genesis ever since it became a prominent market,</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> caching every infected machine’s data and metadata, resulting in over 335,000 unique devices at the time of writing. However, unlike a day-to-day malware analysis assignment, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">we had no specific infection to investigate</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. Since KELA is an external service provider, we learn about Genesis infections affecting our clients from listings on the market and any network or endpoint indicators, leaving us with no access to actual samples.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">So, the first question to answer was:How do you analyze a campaign without traditional indicators of compromise?</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="let-me-guid-you"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Let Me GUID You</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Let’s begin by introducing the components of a typical Genesis listing: the bot metadata, providing context and data on the infected machine; and the data itself – showing the actual stolen credentials and associated details.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" fetchpriority="auto" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20387'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Figure 1: Typical bot listing on the Genesis market" title="Figure 1: Typical bot listing on the Genesis market" width="680" height="387" data-slate-length="0">﻿</span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 1: Typical bot listing on the Genesis market</span></strong></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Notably, Genesis obscures a significant property, which other actors selling infostealer data provide: the infection type. While other markets and sellers clearly indicate which Trojan was used to source the stolen credentials, Genesis prefers white-labeling and rebranding data as their own.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" on a different market, stating it was obtained using Vidar stealer" title="Figure 2: A bot for sale on a different market, stating it was obtained using Vidar stealer" width="680" height="281" ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div><div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0">﻿</span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">With our </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">main area of interest being the attributed stealer</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> – the one data point Genesis actively hides – our methodology focused on trying to extrapolate the available data points. The </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">GUID,</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">module</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> and </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">infection date</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> proved useful in answering that question.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Our preliminary assumption is that the </span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">bot name</span></strong></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> – the title of each Genesis bot – </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">is a globally unique identifier</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, which we will refer to as the bot GUID. To put that into context, note the structure of the bot GUID in Figure 1: it’s composed of five alphanumerical octets separated by hyphens. However, not all Genesis bots follow this distinct pattern; scanning the website, we’ve identified several GUID structures. So, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">if each GUID structure is associated with a particular malware, could we use the patterns to classify infection types</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">?</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" fetchpriority="auto" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20319'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Figure 3: Genesis bots with different GUID patterns" title="Figure 3: Genesis bots with different GUID patterns" width="680" height="319" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x319/32048x960-1739786022311.png" alt="Figure 3: Genesis bots with different GUID patterns" title="Figure 3: Genesis bots with different GUID patterns" width="680" height="319"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-1vclqo4-expandIcon ent-tss-44-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div><div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0">﻿</span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 3: Genesis bots with different GUID patterns</span></strong></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">To answer that, we attempted to </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">group all &gt;335k Genesis bots scraped by KELA into distinct </span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">GUID classes</span></strong></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, based on the structure and syntax observed. We then assigned every GUID group to a malware, effectively mapping the Genesis malware supply chain. Our methodology involved three steps:</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">1. Mapping possible delimiters and splitting each GUID into </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">tokens</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, accordingly;</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">2. Mining seemingly </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">meaningful strings</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> from all tokens into a dictionary; and</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">3. Classifying each GUID based on the </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">length of the token</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> in its GUID and whether an important string appears in any of the tokens.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" fetchpriority="auto" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20419'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Figure 4: KELA’s classification of different GUID patterns in Genesis" title="Figure 4: KELA’s classification of different GUID patterns in Genesis" width="680" height="419" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x419/image76-1739786067846.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" fetchpriority="auto" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x419/image76-1739786067846.png" alt="Figure 4: KELA’s classification of different GUID patterns in Genesis" title="Figure 4: KELA’s classification of different GUID patterns in Genesis" width="680" height="419"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-1vclqo4-expandIcon ent-tss-44-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0">﻿</span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 4: KELA’s classification of different GUID patterns in Genesis</span></strong></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Incorporating this methodology, we grouped all Genesis bots into 340 distinct GUID classes. </span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">That might seem like a major achievement, allowing us to map 340 different GUIDs to known (or unknown!) information stealers. However, as can be seen in Figure 5, many of the GUIDs share another syntactic feature: they end in “_20,” meaning that regardless of how the raw GUID starts, it ends in 20 alphanumeric characters following an underscore.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 5: Initial GUID classes: the grey ones are distinct and well-formed, while the pink ones are less distinct, and were later grouped based on secondary properties</span></strong></em></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="the-four-gui-ders-of-the-apocalypse"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The Four GUIDers of the Apocalypse</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">This insight, along with the meaningful strings appearing in some GUIDs, quickly led us to realize that these GUIDs are essentially a Windows machine name concatenated with a seemingly unique 20-character string. We refer to this class as </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">{MACHINENAME}_20</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. This tweak to the classification methodology allowed us to group over 330 different GUIDs, resulting in </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">four key structures across the entire Genesis dataset.</span></strong></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" fetchpriority="auto" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20401'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Figure 6: Breakdown of bot GUID structures" title="Figure 6: Breakdown of bot GUID structures" width="680" height="401" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x401/6_2-1739786148079.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" fetchpriority="auto" GUID structures" width="680" height="401"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-1vclqo4-expandIcon ent-tss-44-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div><div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 6: Breakdown of bot GUID structures</span></strong></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">With the four malware families classified and the number of infections accounted for, we could decipher other meta-properties and make sense of how Genesis operates. Reviewing infection classes over time suggests </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Genesis actors have been experimenting with different</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Trojans</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">: as seen in Figure 7, during its first year, Genesis only sold infections of </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">{MACHINENAME}_20</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> class, and switched to </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">8-8-8-8-8</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> in late 2018, boosting the number of infected machines obtained by the actors.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" fetchpriority="auto" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20434'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Figure 7: Infection types (by GUID class) over time; dates indicate when the machine was infected" title="Figure 7: Infection types (by GUID class) over time; dates indicate when the machine was infected" width="680" height="434"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-1vclqo4-expandIcon ent-tss-44-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div><div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0">﻿</span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 7: Infection types (by GUID class) over time; dates indicate when the machine was infected</span></strong></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Another interesting date was late 2019 when the </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">32</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> GUID class appeared on the market; Genesis actors might have considered introducing a new malware type into the market, but eventually decided to revert to their leading product – the 8-8-8-8-8 class.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Armed with these insights, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">we set to analyze the </span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">8-8-8-8-8 </span></strong></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">class</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> based on its ongoing ubiquity. In our next post, we’ll dive into the </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><span data-slate-string="true">32</span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> class, providing insights into its short rise and fall on the market, and explore the historical themes of the two remaining categories.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="88888-the-number-of-the-guid"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">88888, the Number of the GUID</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Seeing this pattern,</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true"> our first suspect was AZORult infostealer</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, a malware that </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://threatvector.cylance.com/en_us/home/threat-spotlight-analyzing-azorult-infostealer-malware.html" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">uses a similar GUID</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. However, it was circumstantial at best; we needed some hard evidence linking the two.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Luckily, the cybercrime financial ecosystem is abundant with actors sharing samples of their “logs” – collections obtained via various malware types. We soon came across a prominent MaaS provider operating in a top-tier Russian cybercrime forum. Retrieving some of the data shared by the actor, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">we were able to find official AZORult logs</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> – stolen credentials advertised as having been obtained via AZORult – </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">that share the same GUIDs as Genesis’ </span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">8-8-8-8-8</span></strong></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true"> category</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. data-slate-string="true">8-8-8-8-8 </span></em></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">class, which comprises over 90% of Genesis MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-1vclqo4-expandIcon ent-tss-44-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div><div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0">﻿</span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 9: Genesis listing (top) and an AZORult log obtained from a prominent MaaS provider (bottom) sharing the same GUID and metadata, confirming the “8-8-8-8-8” Genesis GUIDs are indeed AZORult</span></strong></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Operating since 2016, AZORult is a commodity malware widely used by multiple threat actors in numerous campaigns. While its author </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.blueliv.com/cyber-security-and-cyber-threat-intelligence-blog-blueliv/research/azorult-crydbrox-stops-sells-malware-credential-stealer/" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">stopped</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> maintaining the project in late 2018, AZORult is still widespread and used in </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://www.cybereason.com/blog/the-hole-in-the-bucket-attackers-abuse-bitbucket-to-deliver-an-arsenal-of-malware" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">active campaigns</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. AZORult’s source code was readily available to numerous actors who modified the original, producing </span></span></span><a data-slate-node="element" href="https://securityaffairs.co/wordpress/82948/malware/azorult-c-variant.html" rel="nofollow" target="_blank" class="ent-tss-p16xa7-link"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">unofficial variants</span></span></span></a><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> for small-scale, independent campaigns. In the case of Genesis, this could mean actors either operate their own version of the stealer, resell infection from a MaaS provider, or both.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Going back to the AZORult log samples mentioned in Figure 9, we analyzed fifteen months’ worth of records offered by the MaaS provider and found correlating GUIDs and</span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true"> over 10,000 such bots</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. These findings might indicate a </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">supply chain link between Genesis and known cybercriminals</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, where actors monetize their campaigns by selling data to Genesis.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0">﻿</span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 10: Possible cooperation between Genesis and a MaaS provider offering access to AZORult-infected machines</span></strong></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Not only does Genesis use commodity malware for over 90% of its bots – the actors do so as part of a joint business venture with MaaS providers. This offers a glimpse into an interesting aspect of the cybercrime sector: interactions and cooperation between actors. In the case of Genesis, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">old</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">forum posts indicate they’re not entirely independent and are interested in gaining access to compromised machines</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">. As can be seen in Figures 7 and 10, Genesis obviously found a supplier – most likely several – of infections they can utilize for credential-stealing purposes.</span></span></span></p><div class="ent-tss-lr6oy8-imageWrapper"><div data-slate-node="element" data-slate-void="true" style="overflow:hidden;width:100%;display:flex;flex-direction:column;margin-top:30px;align-items:flex-start"><div class="ent-tss-rh3cpz-imageContainer"><img style="width:100% !important" fetchpriority="auto" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="data:image/svg+xml,%3Csvg%20xmlns='http://www.w3.org/2000/svg'%20viewBox='0%200%20680%20339'%3E%3C/svg%3E" alt="Figure 11: A post from the early days of the Genesis Store: “Asking you kindly, if you want to work with us, we need loads in big volumes. Topical infections of 5-10 a week will not do”" title="Figure 11: A post from the early days of the Genesis Store: “Asking you kindly, if you want to work with us, we need loads in big volumes. Topical infections of 5-10 a week will not do”" width="680" height="339" data-lazy-src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x339/11_1-1739786391801.png"><noscript><img style="width:100% !important" loading="lazy" fetchpriority="auto" class="ent-tss-15xthk0-image-image" src="https://entail-assets.com/kelacyber/fit-in/680x339/11_1-1739786391801.png" alt="Figure 11: A post from the early days of the Genesis Store: “Asking you kindly, if you want to work with us, we need loads in big volumes. Topical infections of 5-10 a week will not do”" width="680" height="339"></noscript><button class="MuiButtonBase-root MuiIconButton-root MuiIconButton-sizeMedium ent-tss-1vclqo4-expandIcon ent-tss-44-expandIcon-ref ent-css-1yxmbwk" tabindex="0" type="button"><svg class="MuiSvgIcon-root MuiSvgIcon-fontSizeMedium ent-css-vubbuv" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewbox="0 0 24 24" data-testid="OpenInFullIcon"><path d="M21 11V3h-8l3.29 3.29-10 10L3 13v8h8l-3.29-3.29 10-10z"></path></svg></button></div></div><div data-slate-spacer="true" style="height:0;color:transparent;outline:none;position:absolute"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-zero-width="z" data-slate-length="0">﻿</span></span></span></div></div><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:center"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><em><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Figure 11: A post from the early days of the Genesis Store: “Asking you kindly, if you want to work with us, we need loads in big volumes. Topical infections of 5-10 a week will not do”</span></strong></em></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The next parts of this research will dive deeper into the supply chain relations Genesis has with at least one prominent MaaS provider, shedding light on cybercriminal business models.</span></span></span></p><h2 class="ent-tss-7785c4-h2" color="inherit" id="the-guid-the-bad-and-the-obvious"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">The GUID, the Bad, and the Obvious</span></span></span></h2><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">In the first part of our research, we focused on developing a methodology for continuous monitoring of the Genesis Store – specifically supply-related trends. Analyzing the elements of one of today’s most prolific cybercrime outlets, </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">we were able to link over 300,000 AZORult infections to Genesis</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true"> – or at least to their providers. Behind these numbers are diverse victims: from SMBs to enterprises, from the private sector up to government officials.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">While the fact that a top-tier market uses one of the most popular stealers is hardly surprising, our main point is this: </span></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">Genesis actors are not going anywhere anytime soon, so we might as well keep tabs on them. </span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Gaining a better understanding of the threat, as well as demystifying it, is crucial in establishing KPIs for further monitoring. Only now – when we have clear definitions of the necessary data points to extrapolate from the operations of Genesis actors – can we establish baseline metrics and measure when they’re being disrupted. Adding new malware strains, utilizing new and different credential-stealing modules, or shifting infection volumes can serve as threat indicators to improve awareness.</span></span></span></p><p data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-15cp3ar-paragraph" style="text-align:left"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><strong><span data-slate-string="true">This is also our motivation for the upcoming parts of this research</span></strong></span></span><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">, where we will try to:</span></span></span></p><ul data-slate-node="element" class="ent-tss-1jjvw86-listDisc"><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Identify the three remaining GUID classes, linking them to known malware;</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Describe the different stages of Genesis’ evolution, from inception to a top-tier provider of the cybercriminal underground;</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Deep dive into specific MaaS actors involved, both directly and indirectly, in the Genesis supply chain;</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Explore themes and trends in the MaaS industry and the broader cybercrime financial ecosystem, and their effects on consumers of threat intelligence;</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Refer to biases, traps and gaps in our methodology; and</span></span></span></li><li data-slate-node="element"><span data-slate-node="text"><span data-slate-leaf="true"><span data-slate-string="true">Come up with as many GUID-themed puns as possible.</span></span></span></li></ul></div></div></div></article><div class="ent-tss-1uoc0jk-popularQuestionsWrapper"><div class="ent-tss-165re9h-titleWrapper"><h2 class="MuiTypography-root likely-Russian threat actor operating a successful, borderline innovative, pay-per-bot store since 2018. The following post features a quick-and-easy methodology breaking down over 335,000 unique Genesis infections into four malware groups, allowing us to attribute over 300,000 AZORult infections to the Genesis actors currently involved in campaigns resulting is tens of thousands of new AZORult infections per month. Furthermore, it seems Genesis isn’t necessarily leading these campaigns, but rather working with various Malware-as-a-Service (MaaS) providers and cybercrime services.\nThis discovery, linking Genesis with widely known commodity malware, highlights the ongoing threat to organizations and the proliferation of illegal data obtained from infections. It also sheds light on the supply chain relationships between actors operating within the cybercrime financial ecosystem (read: Dark Net); we’ll explore this theme, including specific actors and trends, in the next posts.\nPreface\nMuch has been written about the Genesis Store: the threat it poses to organizations, the native fingerprinting abilities embedded in the tools it provides, or (shameless plug alert) the shift in cybercrime business models it represents.\nOn a day-to-day operations basis, KELA automatically monitors new listings on the market – allowing our clients to monitor relevant infections. While these real-time alerts allow threat intelligence or incident response teams to remediate relevant threats, many of our clients were interested in a wider, more contextual understanding of Genesis. For example, one report indicated that Genesis “introduced a new breed of stealers specifically designed to collect digital fingerprints and artifacts;” another referred to the Genesis “botnet.” That raises the question, what do Genesis actors actually do? Develop specialized stealers, manage a MaaS botnet, or sell credentials obtained by other actors?\nSeeking to demystify the threat, we focused on three major areas:\nTechnical abilities – which trojan is being used: commodity malware or a tailored tool?Operational independence – are the Genesis actors leading independent campaigns, determining targets and infection methods, or reselling data obtained by other parties?Scale – the spread, infection rate and profits pocketed by the actors\nOur research aims to evaluate the actionable threat level: if ACME Corp found an infected endpoint offered for sale on the market, what should be the next steps of the incident response team? What does a Genesis infection say about further threat hunting, threat containment and lateral movement, or impending payloads? In the age of cybercrime inter-group relations and joint campaigns, understanding how Genesis actors position themselves in the cybercrime financial ecosystem as an emerging threat group can be extremely helpful to defenders.\nLuckily, it’s our business to have access to large cybercrime-related data; as such, KELA’s systems have been scraping Genesis ever since it became a prominent market, caching every infected machine’s data and metadata, resulting in over 335,000 unique devices at the time of writing. However, unlike a day-to-day malware analysis assignment, we had no specific infection to investigate. Since KELA is an external service provider, we learn about Genesis infections affecting our clients from listings on the market and any network or endpoint indicators, leaving us with no access to actual samples.\nSo, the first question to answer was:How do you analyze a campaign without traditional indicators of compromise?\nLet Me GUID You\nLet’s begin by introducing the components of a typical Genesis listing: the bot metadata, providing context and data on the infected machine; and the data itself – showing the actual stolen credentials and associated details.\n\nFigure 1: Typical bot listing on the Genesis market\nNotably, Genesis obscures a significant property, which other actors selling infostealer data provide: the infection type. While other markets and sellers clearly indicate which Trojan was used to source the stolen credentials, Genesis prefers white-labeling and rebranding data as their own.\n\nWith our main area of interest being the attributed stealer – the one data point Genesis actively hides – our methodology focused on trying to extrapolate the available data points. The GUID, module and infection date proved useful in answering that question.\nOur preliminary assumption is that the bot name – the title of each Genesis bot – is a globally unique identifier, which we will refer to as the bot GUID. To put that into context, note the structure of the bot GUID in Figure 1: it’s composed of five alphanumerical octets separated by hyphens. However, not all Genesis bots follow this distinct pattern; scanning the website, we’ve identified several GUID structures. So, if each GUID structure is associated with a particular malware, could we use the patterns to classify infection types?\n\nFigure 3: Genesis bots with different GUID patterns\nTo answer that, we attempted to group all \u003E335k Genesis bots scraped by KELA into distinct GUID classes, based on the structure and syntax observed. We then assigned every GUID group to a malware, effectively mapping the Genesis malware supply chain. Our methodology involved three steps:\n1. Mapping possible delimiters and splitting each GUID into tokens, accordingly;\n2. Mining seemingly meaningful strings from all tokens into a dictionary; and\n3. Classifying each GUID based on the length of the token in its GUID and whether an important string appears in any of the tokens.\n\nFigure 4: KELA’s classification of different GUID patterns in Genesis\nIncorporating this methodology, we grouped all Genesis bots into 340 distinct GUID classes. That might seem like a major achievement, allowing us to map 340 different GUIDs to known (or unknown!) information stealers. However, as can be seen in Figure 5, many of the GUIDs share another syntactic feature: they end in “_20,” meaning that regardless of how the raw GUID starts, it ends in 20 alphanumeric characters following an underscore.\n\nFigure 5: Initial GUID classes: the grey ones are distinct and well-formed, while the pink ones are less distinct, and were later grouped based on secondary properties\nThe Four GUIDers of the Apocalypse\nThis insight, along with the meaningful strings appearing in some GUIDs, quickly led us to realize that these GUIDs are essentially a Windows machine name concatenated with a seemingly unique 20-character string. We refer to this class as {MACHINENAME}_20. This tweak to the classification methodology allowed us to group over 330 different GUIDs, resulting in four key structures across the entire Genesis dataset.\n\nFigure 6: Breakdown of bot GUID structures\nWith the four malware families classified and the number of infections accounted for, we could decipher other meta-properties and make sense of how Genesis operates. Reviewing infection classes over time suggests Genesis actors have been experimenting with different Trojans: as seen in Figure 7, during its first year, Genesis only sold infections of {MACHINENAME}_20 class, and switched to 8-8-8-8-8 in late 2018, boosting the number of infected machines obtained by the actors.\n\nFigure 7: Infection types (by GUID class) over time; dates indicate when the machine was infected\nAnother interesting date was late 2019 when the 32 GUID class appeared on the market; Genesis actors might have considered introducing a new malware type into the market, but eventually decided to revert to their leading product – the 8-8-8-8-8 class.\nArmed with these insights, we set to analyze the 8-8-8-8-8 class based on its ongoing ubiquity. In our next post, we’ll dive into the 32 class, providing insights into its short rise and fall on the market, and explore the historical themes of the two remaining categories.\n88888, the Number of the GUID\nSeeing this pattern, our first suspect was AZORult infostealer, a malware that uses a similar GUID. However, it was circumstantial at best; we needed some hard evidence linking the two.\n\nFigure 8: AZORult’s GUID featuring the 8-8-8-8-8 structure (analyzed by Cylance)\nLuckily, the cybercrime financial ecosystem is abundant with actors sharing samples of their “logs” – collections obtained via various malware types. We soon came across a prominent MaaS provider operating in a top-tier Russian cybercrime forum. Retrieving some of the data shared by the actor, we were able to find official AZORult logs – stolen credentials advertised as having been obtained via AZORult – that share the same GUIDs as Genesis’ 8-8-8-8-8 category. That, along with corresponding metadata, confirmed the 8-8-8-8-8 class, which comprises over 90% of Genesis infections, is based on AZORult.\n\nFigure 9: Genesis listing (top) and an AZORult log obtained from a prominent MaaS provider (bottom) sharing the same GUID and metadata, confirming the “8-8-8-8-8” Genesis GUIDs are indeed AZORult\nOperating since 2016, AZORult is a commodity malware widely used by multiple threat actors in numerous campaigns. While its author stopped maintaining the project in late 2018, AZORult is still widespread and used in active campaigns. AZORult’s source code was readily available to numerous actors who modified the original, producing unofficial variants for small-scale, independent campaigns. In the case of Genesis, this could mean actors either operate their own version of the stealer, resell infection from a MaaS provider, or both.\nGoing back to the AZORult log samples mentioned in Figure 9, we analyzed fifteen months’ worth of records offered by the MaaS provider and found correlating GUIDs and over 10,000 such bots. These findings might indicate a supply chain link between Genesis and known cybercriminals, where actors monetize their campaigns by selling data to Genesis.\n\nFigure 10: Possible cooperation between Genesis and a MaaS provider offering access to AZORult-infected machines\nNot only does Genesis use commodity malware for over 90% of its bots – the actors do so as part of a joint business venture with MaaS providers. This offers a glimpse into an interesting aspect of the cybercrime sector: interactions and cooperation between actors. In the case of Genesis, old forum posts indicate they’re not entirely independent and are interested in gaining access to compromised machines. As can be seen in Figures 7 and 10, Genesis obviously found a supplier – most likely several – of infections they can utilize for credential-stealing purposes.\n\nFigure 11: A post from the early days of the Genesis Store: “Asking you kindly, if you want to work with us, we need loads in big volumes. Topical infections of 5-10 a week will not do”\nThe next parts of this research will dive deeper into the supply chain relations Genesis has with at least one prominent MaaS provider, shedding light on cybercriminal business models.\nThe GUID, the Bad, and the Obvious\nIn the first part of our research, we focused on developing a methodology for continuous monitoring of the Genesis Store – specifically supply-related trends. Analyzing the elements of one of today’s most prolific cybercrime outlets, we were able to link over 300,000 AZORult infections to Genesis – or at least to their providers. Behind these numbers are diverse victims: from SMBs to enterprises, from the private sector up to government officials.\nWhile the fact that a top-tier market uses one of the most popular stealers is hardly surprising, our main point is this: Genesis actors are not going anywhere anytime soon, so we might as well keep tabs on them. Gaining a better understanding of the threat, as well as demystifying it, is crucial in establishing KPIs for further monitoring. Only now – when we have clear definitions of the necessary data points to extrapolate from the operations of Genesis actors – can we establish baseline metrics and measure when they’re being disrupted. Adding new malware strains, utilizing new and different credential-stealing modules, or shifting infection volumes can serve as threat indicators to improve awareness.\nThis is also our motivation for the upcoming parts of this research, where we will try to:\nIdentify the three remaining GUID classes, linking them to known malware;Describe the different stages of Genesis’ evolution, from inception to a top-tier provider of the cybercriminal underground;Deep dive into specific MaaS actors involved, both directly and indirectly, in the Genesis supply chain;Explore themes and trends in the MaaS industry and the broader cybercrime financial ecosystem, and their effects on consumers of threat intelligence;Refer to biases, traps and gaps in our methodology; andCome up with as many GUID-themed puns as possible.","richText":"[{\"type\":\"heading-one\",\"children\":[{\"text\":\"Bottom Line Up Front\"}]},{\"type\":\"paragraph\",\"children\":[{\"text\":\"This is the first post in a series of posts reviewing the supply chain of the Genesis Store market – a likely-Russian threat actor operating a successful, borderline innovative, \"},{\"text\":\"pay-per-bot\",\"italic\":true},{\"text\":\" store since 2018.         // if getParameterByName function is not defined, define it
        if (typeof getParameterByName === 'undefined') {
            function getParameterByName(name, url) {
                if (!url) url = window.location.href;
                name = name.replace(/[\[\]]/g, "\\$&");
                var regex = new RegExp("[?&]" + name + "(=([^&#]*)|&|#|$)"),
                    results = regex.exec(url);
                if (!results) return null;
                if (!results[2]) return '';
                return decodeURIComponent(results[2].replace(/\+/g, " "));
            }
        }

        jQuery(document).ready(function($) {

            // First detect if _GET params are present.
            // We only need utm_source, utm_medium and utm_campaign
            var utm_source = getParameterByName('utm_source');
            var utm_medium = getParameterByName('utm_medium');
            var utm_campaign = getParameterByName('utm_campaign');
            var utm_content = getParameterByName('utm_content');
            var utm_term = getParameterByName('utm_term');

            // Next we will use Cookies.js to set cookies

            // If _GET params are present, set cookies
            if (utm_source) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_source', utm_source, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_medium) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_medium', utm_medium, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_campaign) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_campaign', utm_campaign, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_content) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_content', utm_content, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }
            if (utm_term) {
                Cookies.set('kela_utm_term', utm_term, { expires: 1, path: '/' });
            }

            // Check if cookies are present
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_source')) {
                utm_source = Cookies.get('kela_utm_source');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_medium')) {
                utm_medium = Cookies.get('kela_utm_medium');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_campaign')) {
                utm_campaign = Cookies.get('kela_utm_campaign');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_content')) {
                utm_content = Cookies.get('kela_utm_content');
            }
            if (Cookies.get('kela_utm_term')) {
                utm_term = Cookies.get('kela_utm_term');
            }

            // Wait 4 seconds for the form to load
            setTimeout(function() {
                
                // Find if hidden fields exist, for example: .hs-utm_campaign input
                if ($(".hs-utm_source input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_source input").val(utm_source);
                }
                if ($(".hs-utm_medium input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_medium input").val(utm_medium);
                }   

                if ($(".hs-utm_campaign input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_campaign input").val(utm_campaign);
                }

                if ($(".hs-utm_content input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_content input").val(utm_content);
                }

                if ($(".hs-utm_term input").length) {
                    $(".hs-utm_term input").val(utm_term);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_source input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_source input").val(utm_source);
                }

                if ($(".gfield--type-text.utm_medium input").length) {
                    $(".gfield--type-text.utm_medium input").val(utm_medium);
